<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Natural satellite</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Astronomical body that orbits a planet</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Moons" redirects here. For Earth's natural satellite, see <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Moons_(disambiguation)" title="Moons (disambiguation)">Moons (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pluto_charon_150709_color_final.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Pluto_charon_150709_color_final.png/300px-Pluto_charon_150709_color_final.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Pluto_charon_150709_color_final.png/450px-Pluto_charon_150709_color_final.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Pluto_charon_150709_color_final.png/600px-Pluto_charon_150709_color_final.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pluto_charon_150709_color_final.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Charon_(moon)" title="Charon (moon)">Charon</a> (left) is a natural satellite of <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a> (right), orbiting around a point</div></div></div>
<p>A <b>natural satellite</b> is, in the most common usage, an <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_object" title="Astronomical object">astronomical body</a> that <a href="/wiki/Orbit" title="Orbit">orbits</a> a <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">dwarf planet</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Small_Solar_System_body" title="Small Solar System body">small Solar System body</a> (or sometimes another natural satellite). Natural satellites are often colloquially referred to as <i><b>moons</b></i>, a derivation from the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a> of <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>.
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>, there are six planetary <a href="/wiki/Satellite_system_(astronomy)" title="Satellite system (astronomy)">satellite systems</a> containing 207 known natural satellites altogether. Seven objects commonly considered <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">dwarf planets</a> by astronomers are also known to have natural satellites: <a href="/wiki/90482_Orcus" title="90482 Orcus">Orcus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Pluto" title="Moons of Pluto">Pluto</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Haumea" title="Moons of Haumea">Haumea</a>, <a href="/wiki/50000_Quaoar" title="50000 Quaoar">Quaoar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Makemake" title="Makemake">Makemake</a>, <a href="/wiki/225088_Gonggong" title="225088 Gonggong">Gonggong</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Eris_(dwarf_planet)" title="Eris (dwarf planet)">Eris</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WGPSN_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-WGPSN-1">[1]</a></sup> As of November 2021<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Natural_satellite&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, there are 442 other <a href="/wiki/Minor-planet_moon" title="Minor-planet moon">minor planets known to have natural satellites</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnston_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Johnston-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>A planet usually has at least around 10,000 times the mass of any natural satellites that orbit it, with a correspondingly much larger diameter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%E2%80%93Moon_system" title="Earth–Moon system">Earth–Moon system</a> is a unique exception in the Solar System; at 3,474 km (2,158 miles) across, the Moon is 0.273 times the <a href="/wiki/Diameter" title="Diameter">diameter</a> of Earth and about <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1020198016">.mw-parser-output .frac{white-space:nowrap}.mw-parser-output .frac .num,.mw-parser-output .frac .den{font-size:80%;line-height:0;vertical-align:super}.mw-parser-output .frac .den{vertical-align:sub}.mw-parser-output .sr-only{border:0;clip:rect(0,0,0,0);height:1px;margin:-1px;overflow:hidden;padding:0;position:absolute;width:1px}</style><span class="frac" role="math"><span class="num">1</span>⁄<span class="den">80</span></span> of its mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> The next largest ratios are the <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>–<a href="/wiki/Triton_(moon)" title="Triton (moon)">Triton</a> system at 0.055 (with a mass ratio of about 1 to 5000), the <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>–<a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a> system at 0.044 (with the second mass ratio next to the Earth–Moon system, 1 to 4250), the <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>–<a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">Ganymede</a> system at 0.038, and the <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a>–<a href="/wiki/Titania_(moon)" title="Titania (moon)">Titania</a> system at 0.031.  For the category of <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">dwarf planets</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charon_(moon)" title="Charon (moon)">Charon</a> has the largest ratio, being 0.52 the diameter and 12.2% the mass of <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a>.
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Terminology">Terminology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_satellite&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Terminology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The first known natural satellite was the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>, but it was considered a "planet" until <a href="/wiki/Nicolaus_Copernicus" title="Nicolaus Copernicus">Copernicus</a>' introduction of <i><a href="/wiki/De_revolutionibus_orbium_coelestium" title="De revolutionibus orbium coelestium">De revolutionibus orbium coelestium</a></i> in 1543. Until the discovery of the <a href="/wiki/Galilean_moons" title="Galilean moons">Galilean satellites</a> in 1610 there was no opportunity for referring to such objects as a class. <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo</a> chose to refer to his discoveries as <i>Planetæ</i> ("planets"), but later discoverers chose other terms to distinguish them from the objects they orbited.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The first to use the term <i>satellite</i> to describe orbiting bodies was the German astronomer <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">Johannes Kepler</a> in his pamphlet <i>Narratio de Observatis a se quatuor Iouis satellitibus erronibus</i> ("Narration About Four Satellites of Jupiter Observed") in 1610. He derived the term from the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> word <i>satelles</i>, meaning "guard", "attendant", or "companion", because the <i>satellites</i> accompanied their primary planet in their journey through the heavens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>The term <i>satellite</i> thus became the normal one for referring to an object orbiting a planet, as it avoided the ambiguity of "moon". In 1957, however, the launching of the artificial object <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sputnik" title="Sputnik">Sputnik</a> created a <a href="/wiki/Retronym" title="Retronym">need for new terminology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-5">[5]</a></sup> The terms <i>man-made satellite</i> and <i>artificial moon</i> were very quickly abandoned in favor of the simpler <i>satellite</i>, and as a consequence, the term has become linked primarily with artificial objects flown in space – <span class="cleanup-needed-content" style="padding-left:0.1em; padding-right:0.1em; color:#595959; border:1px solid #DDD;">including, sometimes, even those not in orbit around a planet.</span><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Disputed statement"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (February 2022)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata"> – <a href="/wiki/Talk:Natural_satellite#Dubious" title="Talk:Natural satellite">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Because of this shift in meaning, the term <i>moon</i>, which had continued to be used in a generic sense in works of popular science and in fiction, has regained respectability and is now used interchangeably with <i>natural satellite</i>, even in scientific articles. When it is necessary to avoid both the ambiguity of confusion with Earth's natural satellite the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a> and the natural satellites of the other planets on the one hand, and artificial satellites on the other, the term <i>natural satellite</i> (using "natural" in a sense opposed to "artificial") is used. To further avoid ambiguity, the convention is to capitalize the word Moon when referring to Earth's natural satellite (a <a href="/wiki/Proper_noun" title="Proper noun">proper noun</a>), but not when referring to other natural satellites (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Common_noun" title="Common noun">common nouns</a>).
</p><p>Many authors define "satellite" or "natural satellite" as orbiting some planet or minor planet, synonymous with "moon" – by such a definition all natural satellites are moons, but Earth and other planets are not satellites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>
A few recent authors define "moon" as "a satellite of a planet or minor planet", and "planet" as "a satellite of a star" – such authors consider Earth as a "natural satellite of the Sun".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition_of_a_moon">Definition of a moon</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_satellite&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Definition of a moon">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Moon,_Earth_size_comparison.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1884" data-file-width="2394" decoding="async" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Moon%2C_Earth_size_comparison.jpg/220px-Moon%2C_Earth_size_comparison.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Moon%2C_Earth_size_comparison.jpg/330px-Moon%2C_Earth_size_comparison.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Moon%2C_Earth_size_comparison.jpg/440px-Moon%2C_Earth_size_comparison.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Moon,_Earth_size_comparison.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Size comparison of <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a></div></div></div>
<p>There is no established lower limit on what is considered a "moon". Every natural celestial body with an identified orbit around a planet of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>, some as small as a kilometer across, has been considered a moon, though objects a tenth that size within Saturn's rings, which have not been directly observed, have been called <i><a href="/wiki/Moonlet" title="Moonlet">moonlets</a></i>. Small <a href="/wiki/Minor-planet_moon" title="Minor-planet moon">asteroid moons</a> (natural satellites of asteroids), such as <a href="/wiki/Dactyl_(moon)" title="Dactyl (moon)">Dactyl</a>, have also been called moonlets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marchis05_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marchis05-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>The upper limit is also vague. Two orbiting bodies are sometimes described as a <a href="/wiki/Double_planet" title="Double planet">double planet</a> rather than primary and satellite. <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">Asteroids</a> such as <a href="/wiki/90_Antiope" title="90 Antiope">90 Antiope</a> are considered double asteroids, but they have not forced a clear definition of what constitutes a moon. Some authors consider the Pluto–Charon system to be a double (dwarf) planet. The most common<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> dividing line on what is considered a moon rests upon whether the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Barycentre" title="Barycentre">barycentre</a> is below the surface of the larger body, though this is somewhat arbitrary, because it depends on distance as well as relative mass.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin_and_orbital_characteristics">Origin and orbital characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_satellite&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Origin and orbital characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Circumplanetary_disk" title="Circumplanetary disk">Circumplanetary disk</a></div>
<p>The natural satellites orbiting relatively close to the planet on <a href="/wiki/Retrograde_and_prograde_motion" title="Retrograde and prograde motion">prograde</a>, uninclined circular orbits (<a href="/wiki/Regular_moon" title="Regular moon"><i>regular</i> satellites</a>) are generally thought to have been formed out of the same <a href="/wiki/Circumplanetary_disk" title="Circumplanetary disk">collapsing region</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Protoplanetary_disk" title="Protoplanetary disk">protoplanetary disk</a> that created its primary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arxiv0812_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-arxiv0812-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dangelo_podolak_2015_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-dangelo_podolak_2015-14">[14]</a></sup> In contrast, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Irregular_satellite" title="Irregular satellite">irregular satellites</a> (generally orbiting on distant, <a href="/wiki/Orbital_inclination" title="Orbital inclination">inclined</a>, <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">eccentric</a> and/or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retrograde_motion" title="Retrograde motion">retrograde</a> orbits) are thought to be captured <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroids</a> possibly further fragmented by collisions. Most of the major natural satellites of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> have regular orbits, while most of the small natural satellites have irregular orbits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> and possibly <a href="/wiki/Charon_(moon)" title="Charon (moon)">Charon</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> are exceptions among large bodies in that they are thought to have originated by the collision of two large proto-planetary objects (see the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Giant_impact_hypothesis" title="Giant impact hypothesis">giant impact hypothesis</a>). The material that would have been placed in orbit around the central body is predicted to have reaccreted to form one or more orbiting natural satellites. As opposed to planetary-sized bodies, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asteroid_moon" title="Asteroid moon">asteroid moons</a> are thought to commonly form by this process. <a href="/wiki/Triton_(moon)" title="Triton (moon)">Triton</a> is another exception; although large and in a close, circular orbit, its motion is retrograde and it is thought to be a captured <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">dwarf planet</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Temporary_satellites">Temporary satellites</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_satellite&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Temporary satellites">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Temporary_satellite" title="Temporary satellite">Temporary satellite</a></div>
<p>The capture of an asteroid from a heliocentric orbit is not always permanent. According to simulations, <a href="/wiki/Temporary_satellite" title="Temporary satellite">temporary satellites</a> should be a common phenomenon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carlisle_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carlisle-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fedorets_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fedorets-19">[19]</a></sup> The only observed examples are <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/1991_VG" title="1991 VG">1991 VG</a></span>, <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/2006_RH120" title="2006 RH120">2006 RH<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">120</span></span></a></span>, <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/2020_CD3" title="2020 CD3">2020 CD<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">3</span></span></a></span>.
</p><p><span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/2006_RH120" title="2006 RH120">2006 RH<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">120</span></span></a></span> was a temporary satellite of <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> for nine months in 2006 and 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shefford_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shefford-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sinott_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sinott-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Tidal_locking">Tidal locking</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_satellite&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Tidal locking">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tidal_locking" title="Tidal locking">Tidal locking</a></div>
<p>Most <a href="/wiki/Regular_moon" title="Regular moon">regular moons</a> (natural satellites following relatively close and prograde orbits with small orbital inclination and eccentricity) in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> are tidally locked to their respective primaries, meaning that the same side of the natural satellite always faces its planet. This phenomenon comes about through a loss of energy due to tidal forces raised by the planet, slowing the rotation of the satellite until it is negligible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barnes_2010_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barnes_2010-22">[22]</a></sup> The only known exception is <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>'s natural satellite <a href="/wiki/Hyperion_(moon)" title="Hyperion (moon)">Hyperion</a>, which rotates chaotically because of the gravitational influence of <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a>.
</p><p>In contrast, the outer natural satellites of the giant planets (irregular satellites) are too far away to have become locked. For example, Jupiter's <a href="/wiki/Himalia_(moon)" title="Himalia (moon)">Himalia</a>, Saturn's <a href="/wiki/Phoebe_(moon)" title="Phoebe (moon)">Phoebe</a>, and Neptune's <a href="/wiki/Nereid_(moon)" title="Nereid (moon)">Nereid</a> have rotation periods in the range of ten hours, whereas their orbital periods are hundreds of days.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Satellites_of_satellites">Satellites of satellites</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_satellite&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Satellites of satellites">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Subsatellite" title="Subsatellite">Subsatellite</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rhean_rings_PIA10246_Full_res.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1986" data-file-width="3750" decoding="async" height="117" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Rhean_rings_PIA10246_Full_res.jpg/220px-Rhean_rings_PIA10246_Full_res.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Rhean_rings_PIA10246_Full_res.jpg/330px-Rhean_rings_PIA10246_Full_res.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Rhean_rings_PIA10246_Full_res.jpg/440px-Rhean_rings_PIA10246_Full_res.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rhean_rings_PIA10246_Full_res.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Artist impression of <a href="/wiki/Rhea_(moon)" title="Rhea (moon)">Rhea</a>'s proposed rings</div></div></div>
<p>No "moons of moons" or <a href="/wiki/Subsatellite" title="Subsatellite">subsatellites</a> (natural satellites that orbit a natural satellite of a planet) are currently known. In most cases, the tidal effects of the planet would make such a system unstable.
</p><p>However, calculations performed after the 2008 detection<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> of a possible <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Rhea" title="Rings of Rhea">ring system</a> around Saturn's moon <a href="/wiki/Rhea_(moon)" title="Rhea (moon)">Rhea</a> indicate that satellites orbiting Rhea could have stable orbits. Furthermore, the suspected rings are thought to be narrow,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> a phenomenon normally associated with <a href="/wiki/Shepherd_moon" title="Shepherd moon">shepherd moons</a>. However, targeted images taken by the <i><a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini–Huygens">Cassini</a></i> spacecraft failed to detect rings around Rhea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>It has also been proposed that Saturn's moon <a href="/wiki/Iapetus_(moon)" title="Iapetus (moon)">Iapetus</a> had a satellite in the past; this is one of several hypotheses that have been put forward to account for its <a href="/wiki/Equatorial_ridge_on_Iapetus" title="Equatorial ridge on Iapetus">equatorial ridge</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>Light-curve analysis suggests that Saturn's irregular satellite <a href="/wiki/Kiviuq_(moon)" title="Kiviuq (moon)">Kiviuq</a> is extremely prolate, and is likely a <a href="/wiki/Contact_binary" title="Contact binary">contact binary</a> or even a binary moon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LPSC2654_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-LPSC2654-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Trojan_satellites">Trojan satellites</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_satellite&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Trojan satellites">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Two natural satellites are known to have small companions at both their <a href="/wiki/Lagrange_point#L4_and_L5" title="Lagrange point">L<sub>4</sub></a> and <a href="/wiki/Lagrange_point#L4_and_L5" title="Lagrange point">L<sub>5</sub></a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lagrangian_point" title="Lagrangian point">Lagrangian points</a>, sixty degrees ahead and behind the body in its orbit. These companions are called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trojan_moon" title="Trojan moon">trojan moons</a>, as their orbits are analogous to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trojan_asteroid" title="Trojan asteroid">trojan asteroids</a> of <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>. The trojan moons are <a href="/wiki/Telesto_(moon)" title="Telesto (moon)">Telesto</a> and <a href="/wiki/Calypso_(moon)" title="Calypso (moon)">Calypso</a>, which are the leading and following companions, respectively, of the Saturnian moon <a href="/wiki/Tethys_(moon)" title="Tethys (moon)">Tethys</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Helene_(moon)" title="Helene (moon)">Helene</a> and <a href="/wiki/Polydeuces_(moon)" title="Polydeuces (moon)">Polydeuces</a>, the leading and following companions of the Saturnian moon <a href="/wiki/Dione_(moon)" title="Dione (moon)">Dione</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Asteroid_satellites">Asteroid satellites</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_satellite&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Asteroid satellites">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asteroid_moon" title="Asteroid moon">Asteroid moon</a></div>
<p>The discovery of <a href="/wiki/243_Ida" title="243 Ida">243 Ida</a>'s natural satellite <a href="/wiki/Dactyl_(moon)" title="Dactyl (moon)">Dactyl</a> in the early 1990s confirmed that some <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroids</a> have natural satellites; indeed, <a href="/wiki/87_Sylvia" title="87 Sylvia">87 Sylvia</a> has two. Some, such as <a href="/wiki/90_Antiope" title="90 Antiope">90 Antiope</a>, are double asteroids with two comparably sized components.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Shape">Shape</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_satellite&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Shape">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Satellite_planet" title="Satellite planet">Satellite planet</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Masses_of_all_moons_in_the_Solar_System.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="651" data-file-width="752" decoding="async" height="260" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Masses_of_all_moons_in_the_Solar_System.png/300px-Masses_of_all_moons_in_the_Solar_System.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Masses_of_all_moons_in_the_Solar_System.png/450px-Masses_of_all_moons_in_the_Solar_System.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Masses_of_all_moons_in_the_Solar_System.png/600px-Masses_of_all_moons_in_the_Solar_System.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Masses_of_all_moons_in_the_Solar_System.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The relative masses of the natural satellites of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>. <a href="/wiki/Mimas_(moon)" title="Mimas (moon)">Mimas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Enceladus" title="Enceladus">Enceladus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Miranda_(moon)" title="Miranda (moon)">Miranda</a> are too small to be visible at this scale. All the irregularly shaped natural satellites, even added together, would also be too small to be visible.</div></div></div>
<p>Neptune's moon <a href="/wiki/Proteus_(moon)" title="Proteus (moon)">Proteus</a> is the largest irregularly shaped natural satellite; the shapes of Eris' moon <a href="/wiki/Dysnomia_(moon)" title="Dysnomia (moon)">Dysnomia</a> and Orcus' moon <a href="/wiki/Vanth_(moon)" title="Vanth (moon)">Vanth</a> are unknown. All other known natural satellites that are at least the size of Uranus's <a href="/wiki/Miranda_(moon)" title="Miranda (moon)">Miranda</a> have lapsed into rounded <a href="/wiki/Ellipsoid" title="Ellipsoid">ellipsoids</a> under <a href="/wiki/Hydrostatic_equilibrium" title="Hydrostatic equilibrium">hydrostatic equilibrium</a>, i.e. are "round/rounded satellites" and are sometimes categorized as <a href="/wiki/Planetary-mass_moon" title="Planetary-mass moon">planetary-mass moons</a>. The larger natural satellites, being tidally locked, tend toward <a href="/wiki/Oval" title="Oval">ovoid</a> (egg-like) shapes: squat at their poles and with longer equatorial axes in the direction of their primaries (their planets) than in the direction of their motion. Saturn's moon <a href="/wiki/Mimas_(moon)" title="Mimas (moon)">Mimas</a>, for example, has a major axis 9% greater than its polar axis and 5% greater than its other equatorial axis. <a href="/wiki/Methone_(moon)" title="Methone (moon)">Methone</a>, another of Saturn's moons, is only around 3 km in diameter and <a href="/wiki/File:Methone_PIA14633.jpg" title="File:Methone PIA14633.jpg">visibly egg-shaped</a>. The effect is smaller on the largest natural satellites, where their own gravity is greater relative to the effects of tidal distortion, especially those that orbit less massive planets or, as in the case of the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>, at greater distances.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geological_activity">Geological activity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_satellite&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Geological activity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<p>Of the nineteen known natural satellites in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> that are large enough to have lapsed into <a href="/wiki/Hydrostatic_equilibrium" title="Hydrostatic equilibrium">hydrostatic equilibrium</a>, several remain geologically active today. <a href="/wiki/Io_(moon)" title="Io (moon)">Io</a> is the most volcanically active body in the Solar System, while <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">Europa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Enceladus" title="Enceladus">Enceladus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Triton_(moon)" title="Triton (moon)">Triton</a> display evidence of ongoing <a href="/wiki/Tectonics" title="Tectonics">tectonic activity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cryovolcano" title="Cryovolcano">cryovolcanism</a>. In the first three cases, the geological activity is powered by the <a href="/wiki/Tidal_heating#Tidal_heating" title="Tidal heating">tidal heating</a> resulting from having <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">eccentric orbits</a> close to their giant-planet primaries. (This mechanism would have also operated on Triton in the past, before its orbit was <a href="/wiki/Tidal_circularization" title="Tidal circularization">circularized</a>.) Many other natural satellites, such as Earth's <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">Ganymede</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tethys_(moon)" title="Tethys (moon)">Tethys</a> and <a href="/wiki/Miranda_(moon)" title="Miranda (moon)">Miranda</a>, show evidence of past geological activity, resulting from energy sources such as the <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">decay</a> of their <a href="/wiki/Primordial_nuclide" title="Primordial nuclide">primordial</a> <a href="/wiki/Radionuclide" title="Radionuclide">radioisotopes</a>, greater past orbital eccentricities (due in some cases to past <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance" title="Orbital resonance">orbital resonances</a>), or the <a href="/wiki/Planetary_differentiation" title="Planetary differentiation">differentiation</a> or freezing of their interiors. Enceladus and <a href="/wiki/Triton_(moon)" title="Triton (moon)">Triton</a> both have active features resembling <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geysers" title="Geysers">geysers</a>, although in the case of Triton solar heating appears to provide the energy. Titan and Triton have significant atmospheres; Titan also has <a href="/wiki/Lakes_of_Titan" title="Lakes of Titan">hydrocarbon lakes</a>. Also Io and Callisto have atmospheres, even if they are extremely thin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Four of the largest natural satellites, <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">Europa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">Ganymede</a>, <a href="/wiki/Callisto_(moon)" title="Callisto (moon)">Callisto</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a>, are thought to have subsurface oceans of liquid water, while smaller Enceladus may have localized subsurface liquid water.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence_in_the_Solar_System">Occurrence in the Solar System</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_satellite&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Occurrence in the Solar System">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Euler_diagram_of_solar_system_bodies.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="490" data-file-width="635" decoding="async" height="270" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Euler_diagram_of_solar_system_bodies.svg/350px-Euler_diagram_of_solar_system_bodies.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Euler_diagram_of_solar_system_bodies.svg/525px-Euler_diagram_of_solar_system_bodies.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Euler_diagram_of_solar_system_bodies.svg/700px-Euler_diagram_of_solar_system_bodies.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Euler_diagram_of_solar_system_bodies.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Euler_diagram" title="Euler diagram">Euler diagram</a> showing the types of bodies in the Solar System.</div></div></div>
<p>Beside planets and dwarf planets objects within our Solar System known to have natural satellites are 76 in the <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">asteroid belt</a> (five with two each), four <a href="/wiki/Jupiter_trojan" title="Jupiter trojan">Jupiter trojans</a>, 39 <a href="/wiki/Near-Earth_object" title="Near-Earth object">near-Earth objects</a> (two with two satellites each), and 14 <a href="/wiki/List_of_Mars-crossing_minor_planets" title="List of Mars-crossing minor planets">Mars-crossers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnston_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Johnston-2">[2]</a></sup> There are also 84 known natural satellites of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trans-Neptunian_objects" title="Trans-Neptunian objects">trans-Neptunian objects</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnston_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-Johnston-2">[2]</a></sup> Some 150 additional small bodies have been observed within the <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Saturn" title="Rings of Saturn">rings of Saturn</a>, but only a few were tracked long enough to establish orbits. Planets around other stars are likely to have satellites as well, and although numerous candidates have been detected to date, none have yet been confirmed.
</p><p>Of the inner planets, <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a> and <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a> have no natural satellites; Earth has one large natural satellite, known as the Moon; and <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> has two tiny natural satellites, <a href="/wiki/Phobos_(moon)" title="Phobos (moon)">Phobos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Deimos_(moon)" title="Deimos (moon)">Deimos</a>.
The <a href="/wiki/Giant_planet" title="Giant planet">giant planets</a> have extensive systems of natural satellites, including half a dozen comparable in size to Earth's Moon: the four <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galilean_moon" title="Galilean moon">Galilean moons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Triton_(moon)" title="Triton (moon)">Triton</a>. Saturn has an additional six mid-sized natural satellites massive enough to have achieved <a href="/wiki/Hydrostatic_equilibrium" title="Hydrostatic equilibrium">hydrostatic equilibrium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a> has five. It has been suggested that some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_satellite_habitability" title="Natural satellite habitability">satellites may potentially harbour life</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WRD-20150127_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-WRD-20150127-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>Among the objects generally agreed by astronomers to be dwarf planets, <a href="/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet)" title="Ceres (dwarf planet)">Ceres</a> and <a href="/wiki/90377_Sedna" title="90377 Sedna">Sedna</a> have no known natural satellites. Pluto has the relatively large natural satellite Charon and four smaller natural satellites; <a href="/wiki/Styx_(moon)" title="Styx (moon)">Styx</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nix_(moon)" title="Nix (moon)">Nix</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kerberos_(moon)" title="Kerberos (moon)">Kerberos</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hydra_(moon)" title="Hydra (moon)">Hydra</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esahubble_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-esahubble-30">[30]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Haumea" title="Haumea">Haumea</a> has two natural satellites; <a href="/wiki/90482_Orcus" title="90482 Orcus">Orcus</a>, <a href="/wiki/50000_Quaoar" title="50000 Quaoar">Quaoar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Makemake" title="Makemake">Makemake</a>, <a href="/wiki/225088_Gonggong" title="225088 Gonggong">Gonggong</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Eris_(dwarf_planet)" title="Eris (dwarf planet)">Eris</a> have one each. The Pluto–Charon system is unusual in that the <a href="/wiki/Center_of_mass#Barycenter_in_astrophysics_and_astronomy" title="Center of mass">center of mass</a> lies in open space between the two, a characteristic sometimes associated with a <a href="/wiki/Double_planet" title="Double planet">double-planet</a> system.
</p><p>The seven largest <a href="/wiki/List_of_natural_satellites" title="List of natural satellites">natural satellites</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> (those bigger than 2,500 km across) are <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galilean_moon" title="Galilean moon">Galilean moons</a> (<a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">Ganymede</a>, <a href="/wiki/Callisto_(moon)" title="Callisto (moon)">Callisto</a>, <a href="/wiki/Io_(moon)" title="Io (moon)">Io</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">Europa</a>), Saturn's moon Titan, Earth's <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">moon</a>, and Neptune's captured natural satellite <a href="/wiki/Triton_(moon)" title="Triton (moon)">Triton</a>. Triton, the smallest of these, has more mass than all smaller natural satellites together. Similarly in the next size group of nine mid-sized natural satellites, between 1,000 km and 1,600 km across, <a href="/wiki/Titania_(moon)" title="Titania (moon)">Titania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oberon_(moon)" title="Oberon (moon)">Oberon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rhea_(moon)" title="Rhea (moon)">Rhea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iapetus_(moon)" title="Iapetus (moon)">Iapetus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charon_(moon)" title="Charon (moon)">Charon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ariel_(moon)" title="Ariel (moon)">Ariel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Umbriel_(moon)" title="Umbriel (moon)">Umbriel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dione_(moon)" title="Dione (moon)">Dione</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tethys_(moon)" title="Tethys (moon)">Tethys</a>, the smallest, Tethys, has more mass than all smaller natural satellites together. As well as the natural satellites of the various planets, there are also over 80 known natural satellites of the <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">dwarf planets</a>, <a href="/wiki/Minor_planet" title="Minor planet">minor planets</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Small_Solar_System_body" title="Small Solar System body">small Solar System bodies</a>. Some studies estimate that up to 15% of all trans-Neptunian objects could have satellites.
</p><p>The following is a comparative table classifying the natural satellites in the Solar System by diameter. The column on the right includes some notable planets, dwarf planets, asteroids, and trans-Neptunian objects for comparison. The natural satellites of the planets are named after mythological figures. These are predominantly Greek, except for the <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Uranus" title="Moons of Uranus">Uranian natural satellites</a>, which are named after Shakespearean characters. The nineteen bodies massive enough to have achieved hydrostatic equilibrium are in bold in the table below. Minor planets and satellites suspected but not proven to have achieved a hydrostatic equilibrium are italicized in the table below.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_satellite&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r998391716">.mw-parser-output .div-col{margin-top:0.3em;column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .div-col-small{font-size:90%}.mw-parser-output .div-col-rules{column-rule:1px solid #aaa}.mw-parser-output .div-col dl,.mw-parser-output .div-col ol,.mw-parser-output .div-col ul{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .div-col li,.mw-parser-output .div-col dd{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}</style><div class="div-col" style="column-width: 30em;">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Circumplanetary_disk" title="Circumplanetary disk">Circumplanetary disk</a> – Accumulation of matter around a planet</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Co-orbital_moon" title="Co-orbital moon">Co-orbital moon</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Exomoon" title="Exomoon">Exomoon</a> – Moon beyond the Solar System</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Inner_moon" title="Inner moon">Inner moon</a> – Natural satellite orbiting inside the orbit of a larger moon</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Irregular_moon" title="Irregular moon">Irregular moon</a> – Captured satellite following an irregular orbit</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/List_of_natural_satellites" title="List of natural satellites">List of natural satellites</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Naming_of_moons" title="Naming of moons">Naming of moons</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Quasi-satellite" title="Quasi-satellite">Quasi-satellite</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Subsatellite" title="Subsatellite">Subsatellite</a> – A satellite that orbits a natural satellite (aka Moonmoon)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_discovery_of_Solar_System_planets_and_their_moons" title="Timeline of discovery of Solar System planets and their moons">Timeline of discovery of Solar System planets and their moons</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trojan_moon" title="Trojan moon">Trojan moon</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tug_of_war_(astronomy)" title="Tug of war (astronomy)">Tug of war (astronomy)</a> – Ratio of gravitational forces on a satellite from the Sun and host planet</li></ul></div>
